Times News Service  DOES DAVE NEED ANOTHER TOOTH?  A young associate account manager at D'Arcy Masius Benton &AMP; Bowles in New York has had a ``brush with greatness'' _ figuratively and literally.  Molly Glover, 24, who joined the agency a year and a half ago and works on the Crest toothpaste account, appeared on ``Late Show With David Letterman'' on Monday night. Her visit was part of a stunt stemming from an oversized toothbrush and tooth that decorate the window of her office, which is visible to members of the Letterman staff from their midtown Manhattan studio.  Letterman called Ms. Glover, asking her to bring the props to the studio, where he used them to comic effect, squeezing gobs of Crest on the toothbrush and sending Ms. Glover onto the street to be doused by a hose.  ``It was a surprise for me,'' Ms. Glover said on Tuesday. ``I was more excited than nervous.'' Asked if she would like to become one of the show's many running gags, she replied, ``I would like for him to call me again, but I have no idea if he will.'' She may well return; Letterman kept the tooth, and she taped a sign to her window yesterday reading: ``Dave, Where's my tooth? Molly.''  Jay Schwartz, a spokesman for the Procter &AMP; Gamble Co. in Cincinnati, which makes Crest, said: ``Being in the toothpaste and toothbrush business, we love to see people smile and laugh. We thought it was funny, silly and great exposure for Crest.''  FIFI AWARDS NOTE FRAGRANCE ADS  The Fragrance Foundation presented its 23rd annual Fifi Awards on Tuesday night in New York for the best fragrance advertising campaigns for the year.  Jean-Paul Gaultier Mode et Parfums won two awards for 1994 for its new women's fragrance, one for the best national print campaign and the other for the best television campaign; both were created in-house.  Halston Borghese International won for the best national print campaign for a men's fragrance; its ads for Catalyst for Men were created by Lowe &AMP; Partners/SMS in New York, a unit of the Interpublic Group of Cos. And Paco Rabanne Parfums, an affiliate of Puig, won for the best national television campaign for a men's fragrance; the spots for its new XS brand were created by Ogilvy &AMP; Mather New York, a unit of the WPP Group.  WHITE, GOOD BUYS LUCA STEPHAN  White, Good &AMP; Co. in Lebanon, Pa., said on Tuesday that it had acquired Luca Stephan Partners in New York, and renamed it White Good Luca. The terms were not disclosed.  Luca Stephan's billings are estimated at $3.5 million; its clients include The Financial Times, owned by Pearson PLC, and Gruner &AMP; Jahr USA Publishing, a unit of Bertelsmann AG. White, Good, with divisions specializing in public relations and design, has estimated billings of $9 million; its clients include Wyeth-Ayerst International and the Wilmington Savings Fund Society.  Frank Luca, 39, creative director of Luca Stephan, will head White Good Luca as creative director. George Stephan, 40, who was president of Luca Stephan, is expected to announce soon his plans to join another agency.  CHRISTOPHER VINCENT IN VENTURE WITH FGI  FGI Inc. in Chapel Hill, N.C., and Christopher Vincent Inc. in New York said on Tuesday that they had formed a joint venture under which FGI has acquired a 50 percent stake in Vincent, and Christopher Vincent has acquired a minority interest in FGI. The terms were not disclosed.  As part of the deal, Christopher Vincent will be renamed FGI New York. Tom Butta, 38, president and chief executive there, will head FGI New York as president and chief executive. Christopher Vincent, founded in 1987, has billings estimated at $13 million; its clients include KPMG Peat Marwick and the James River Corp. FGI, founded in 1982, has billings estimated at $56 million; clients include GTE Mobilnet and Interactive Magic.  ACCOUNTS  Westfield Corp., Los Angeles, to Foote, Cone &AMP; Belding, Los Angeles, a unit of True North Communications, as the first agency to handle the consolidated account of the operator of shopping centers. The billings were estimated at $5 million.  Kelliher/Samets/Volk, Burlington, Vt., was awarded three accounts with combined billings estimated at $1.2 million: Cellular One, South Burlington, Vt., to handle advertising in Vermont and parts of New Hampshire, previously handled by the Hayes Group, Williston, Vt.; Green Mountain Power, South Burlington, Vt., previously handled in-house, and Camp Echo Lake, Warrensburg, N.Y., also previously in-house.  Heinz U.S.A., Pittsburgh, a division of the H.J. Heinz Co., to Dymun-Nelson &AMP; Co., Pittsburgh, to handle advertising for Heinz specialty sauce brands, including chili sauce and seafood cocktail sauce. The accounts of those brands, long inactive, were previously handled by the Leo Burnett Co., Chicago, which resigned from them by mutual agreement in November. The billings were not disclosed.  Knowledge Adventure Inc., La Crescenta, Calif., to Kalis &AMP; Savage, Pacific Palisades, Calif., to handle the account of the educational software company, previously at Stein Robaire Helm, Los Angeles, now Kresser Stein Robaire. The billings have not been determined.  PEOPLE  Jack Deitchman, senior partner and U.S. director for broadcast and programming at Ogilvy &AMP; Mather New York, a unit of the WPP Group, has resigned to join Wordsworth Associates, Emerson, N.J., in the new position of managing partner. No successor has been named at Ogilvy New York.  MISCELLANY  Publishers Information Bureau, New York, reported that magazine advertising pages in May rose 6.1 percent, to 18,502, from May 1994. Times News Service No. 1717 Wed., Oct. 25 (NYLite items can be published if desired; please credit the contributor. Contribute to NYLite and get a NYT rolling-writer style pen.)  DAVID LETTERMAN TOP TEN  ``Fidel Castro Pick-Up Lines''  10. ``I have no hard currency, but if I did could I buy you a drink?''  9. ``You've started a revolution in my pants.''  8. ``Want to come back to my crumbling palace for a 10-year-old tin of Soviet Sardines?''  7. ``When I look at you I get a `Cuban Missile Crisis.'''  6. ``You are almost as gorgeous as `Three's Company's' Joyce Dewitt.'' (Dewitt exits theater.)  5. ``Would you like to join me for a little class struggle between the sheets?''  4. ``You may never get another chance to do it with a commie.''  3. ``Would you mind checking my beard for ticks?''  2. ``These day-long beard lines are full of phonies _ let's go someplace quiet.''  1. ``I can't spell Cuba without `U.'''  WORST SONGS EVER!... CONTINUED.  An anonymous contributor, suggesting that he/she has a foot (feet?) in the doors of all musical decades back to the 50s, adds the following:  ``Bobby's Girl.'' _ Marcie Blaine.  ``Lightning's Striking Again.'' _ Lou Christie.  ``Witch Doctor.'' _ David Seville.  ``Mother-in-Law.'' _ Ernie K. Doe.  ``Bang, Bang'' _ Cher.  ``Bobby Sox to Stockings'' _ Frankie Avalon.  ``Turn me Loose'' _ Fabian.  ``Be True to Your School'' _ Beach Boys  ``Little Old Lady From Pasadena'' _ Beach Boys  ``She' So Fine, My 409'' _ Artist unknown.  ``Uptown Girl'' _ Billy Joel.  ``My Dad'' _ Paul Peterson.  ``Town Without Pity.'' _ Gene Pitney  ``Deck of Cards.'' _ Wink Martindale.  ``Rag Doll.'' _ Four Seasons.  ``Chances Are'' _ Johnny Mathis.  ``If there is an annoying song that should be considered for the `Oldies, But Baddies' list, it is Cher's `Gypsies, Tramps and Thieves.' Worst song in the last decade? A tie between Vanilla Ice's `Ice, Ice Baby' and the B-52's `Love Shack.''  _Mike Rogers, sports editor at the Orange (Texas) Leader.  From Belinda Paschal, entertainment writer at the Antelope Valley (Calif.) Press:  ``You wanna talk about worst songs of the century? It would take longer than the O.J. trial to recall ALL the insipid song lyrics that belong in the Music Hall of Shame. Here are just a few:  ``I've been to paradise, but I've never been to me.''  _``I've Been to Paradise'' by Charlene.  ``Midnight at the oasis/Sing your camel to bed.''  _``Midnight at the Oasis'' by Maria Muldaur.  ``De Doo Doo Da, De Da Da Da ... is all I want to say to you.''  _``De Doo Doo Doo, De da Da Da,'' by The Police.  BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Uncle Sam may run out of money soon. Yes, but the money he'll run out of is what he borrowed because he was out of money.  The U.N. birthday party was called the largest gathering of world leaders in history. By contrast, the recent Clinton-Yeltsin meeting was a gathering of the largest world leaders.  When Boris Yeltsin told the White House press corps, ``You're a disaster,'' the other day, Bill Clinton laughed so hard he cried. But notice he didn't assign FEMA to the case.  People in Quebec, who long have talked of splitting from Canada, vote Monday on it. The separatists' motto: ``If at first you don't secede. . .''  At a separatist rally in Quebec City the other night, people chanted, ``We want a country.'' Aren't pieces of the old Soviet Union, say, for sale?  ``There are no death squads in Cuba,'' Fidel Castro told New Yorkers the other day. Apparently this is what passes for good news there.  No doubt you will always remember exactly where you were and what you were doing when you heard that Pete Wilson had endorsed Bob Dole.  Thanksgiving turkeys this year will cost a bit more than they did last year, officials say. We report this so you may keep abreast.  Strong export demand for U.S. turkeys is keeping prices up, reports say. Is this a global _ or gobble _ economy?  The rocket that blew up just after launch from a NASA facility the other day was worth $20 million, it's reported. Which works out now to about a dollar a piece.  CITY STREETS  SCENE: A theater district boite.  Time: Late Saturday night, during the weekend of the United Nations celebration.  Dramatis personae: Three young male theater types, very likely dancers, discussing rumor and fact.  Fellow one: I heard Yeltsin was at ``Sunset'' tonight.  Fellow two: The Foreign Minister of Egypt and the King of Latvia were at ``Victor/Victoria.''  Fellow three, quietly: I saw my periodontist and his wife in Shubert Alley.  David Snyder, a native New Yorker who has been living in Baltimore, paid a recent return visit. In the lobby of the Empire State Building, a tourist asked him for directions to Madison Avenue. ``That way,'' Snyder said, pointing west. No sooner had the words left his mouth than he realized he had misguided her, but she was swallowed up in the tumult of 34th Street.  Several hours later, as Snyder strolled through Central Park, a vaguely familiar face stared at him steadily, and a voice said icily: ``Thanks a lot!''  You can't hide in New York.  Crowds lined up along West 42d Street waiting for Pope John Paul II to return from Giants Stadium through the Lincoln Tunnel. As the papal entourage came slowly into view, a buzz of excitement took hold and people began to wave and shout. As the Popemobile passed them, people smiled. The Pope seemed to be smiling, too, and it could have been because a group of diners from Chez Josephine fervently waved their oversize dinner napkins in his direction.  And then, he was gone and everyone stood in silence.  When the diners returned to their meal at Chez Jo, one of them approached Jean-Claude Baker, the proprietor.  ``We're not Catholic,'' she said, ``but we were so moved by the Pope that'' _a pause here _ ``well, may we keep our napkins?''  From Barbara Ball Buff:  I've always known that New York is a city of specialists. Just how special I didn't realize until the other afternoon.  I went to the Upper East Side market across the street from where I live, intending to give a bag of empty bottles and cans to the neighborhood collectors. This time none of them were there and the bottle-recycling machines were out of order. I wound up carrying the bags back across the street; there, I encountered a regular walking along with a large bag bulging with empty bottles. Holding open my bag of a dozen or so plastic bottles, I asked him, ``Would you like these?'''  Without pausing and with only a quick glance into the bag, he replied, ``I don't do that kind.''  After recently viewing the new Jurassic-size refurbishment of the dinosaur exhibition hall at the American Museum of Natural History, Dr. William G. Cahan, a cancer specialist as well as a fellow with an antic sense of humor, came up with several species.  The musical: Dinahshorus.  The literary: Brontesaurus and Rogetthesaurus.  Diary readers, take it from there.  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) At a separatist rally in Quebec City the other night, people chanted, ``We want a country.'' Aren't pieces of the old Soviet Union, say, for sale?  ``There are no death squads in Cuba,'' Fidel Castro told New Yorkers the other day. Apparently this is what passes for good news there.  No doubt you will always remember exactly where you were and what you were doing when you heard that Pete Wilson had endorsed Bob Dole.  Thanksgiving turkeys this year will cost a bit more than they did last year, officials say. We report this so you may keep abreast.  Strong export demand for U.S. turkeys is keeping prices up, reports say. Is this a global _ or gobble _ economy?  The rocket that blew up just after launch from a NASA facility the other day was worth $20 million, it's reported. Which works out now to about a dollar a piece.  CITY STREETS  SCENE: A theater district boite.  Time: Late Saturday night, during the weekend of the United Nations celebration.  Dramatis personae: Three young male theater types, very likely dancers, discussing rumor and fact.  Fellow one: I heard Yeltsin was at ``Sunset'' tonight.  Fellow two: The Foreign Minister of Egypt and the King of Latvia were at ``Victor/Victoria.''  Fellow three, quietly: I saw my periodontist and his wife in Shubert Alley.  David Snyder, a native New Yorker who has been living in Baltimore, paid a recent return visit. In the lobby of the Empire State Building, a tourist asked him for directions to Madison Avenue. ``That way,'' Snyder said, pointing west. No sooner had the words left his mouth than he realized he had misguided her, but she was swallowed up in the tumult of 34th Street.  Several hours later, as Snyder strolled through Central Park, a vaguely familiar face stared at him steadily, and a voice said icily: ``Thanks a lot!''  You can't hide in New York.  Crowds lined up along West 42d Street waiting for Pope John Paul II to return from Giants Stadium through the Lincoln Tunnel. As the papal entourage came slowly into view, a buzz of excitement took hold and people began to wave and shout. As the Popemobile passed them, people smiled. The Pope seemed to be smiling, too, and it could have been because a group of diners from Chez Josephine fervently waved their oversize dinner napkins in his direction.  And then, he was gone and everyone stood in silence.  When the diners returned to their meal at Chez Jo, one of them approached Jean-Claude Baker, the proprietor.  ``We're not Catholic,'' she said, ``but we were so moved by the Pope that'' _a pause here _ ``well, may we keep our napkins?''  From Barbara Ball Buff:  I've always known that New York is a city of specialists. Just how special I didn't realize until the other afternoon.  I went to the Upper East Side market across the street from where I live, intending to give a bag of empty bottles and cans to the neighborhood collectors. This time none of them were there and the bottle-recycling machines were out of order. I wound up carrying the bags back across the street; there, I encountered a regular walking along with a large bag bulging with empty bottles. Holding open my bag of a dozen or so plastic bottles, I asked him, ``Would you like these?'''  Without pausing and with only a quick glance into the bag, he replied, ``I don't do that kind.''  After recently viewing the new Jurassic-size refurbishment of the dinosaur exhibition hall at the American Museum of Natural History, Dr. William G. Cahan, a cancer specialist as well as a fellow with an antic sense of humor, came up with several species.  The musical: Dinahshorus.  The literary: Brontesaurus and Rogetthesaurus.  Diary readers, take it from there.  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) DAVID LETTERMAN TOP TEN  ``Fidel Castro Pick-Up Lines''  10. ``I have no hard currency, but if I did could I buy you a drink?''  9. ``You've started a revolution in my pants.''  8. ``Want to come back to my crumbling palace for a 10-year-old tin of Soviet Sardines?''  7. ``When I look at you I get a `Cuban Missile Crisis.'''  6. ``You are almost as gorgeous as `Three's Company's' Joyce Dewitt.'' (Dewitt exits theater.)  5. ``Would you like to join me for a little class struggle between the sheets?''  4. ``You may never get another chance to do it with a commie.''  3. ``Would you mind checking my beard for ticks?''  2. ``These day-long beard lines are full of phonies _ let's go someplace quiet.''  1. ``I can't spell Cuba without `U.''' For Thursday:  WASHINGTON NEWS (``w'' category)  DEBT-CEILING (Washington) _ A number of Republicans who used to attack Democrats for voting to raise the national debt are expected to swallow hard Thursday and do the same thing themselves. The House vote on the debt ceiling will come when it passes its package to balance the federal budget in seven years. By ELIZABETH SCHWINN.  INTERNATIONAL NEWS (``i'' category)  EUROPE-WELFARE (Paris) _ Alarmed Europeans have begun wrestling with the problem of how to maintain their high standard of living and all-embracing social welfare systems in a global economy increasingly dominated by efficient, low-cost industries in Asian countries that do not offer the same social guarantees. By BERNARD D. KAPLAN.  For Sunday:  COMMENTARY (``k'' category)  THOMPSON-COLUMN (Washington) _ The meeting of Presidents Clinton and Yeltsen at Hyde Park as the United Nations celebrated its 50th anniversary conjured up memories of the magical days of World War II when the United States and the Soviet Union were close allies. But like the U.S.-Russian relationship, the course followed by the United Nations has been rocky and often disheartening. By ROBERT E. DAVID LETTERMAN TOP TEN  ``Fidel Castro Pick-Up Lines''  10. ``I have no hard currency, but if I did could I buy you a drink?''  9. ``You've started a revolution in my pants.''  8. ``Want to come back to my crumbling palace for a 10-year-old tin of Soviet Sardines?''  7. ``When I look at you I get a `Cuban Missile Crisis.'''  6. ``You are almost as gorgeous as `Three's Company's' Joyce Dewitt.'' (Dewitt exits theater.)  5. ``Would you like to join me for a little class struggle between the sheets?''  4. ``You may never get another chance to do it with a commie.''  3. ``Would you mind checking my beard for ticks?''  2. ``These day-long beard lines are full of phonies _ let's go someplace quiet.''  1. ``I can't spell Cuba without `U.''' Worst song in the last decade? A tie between Vanilla Ice's `Ice, Ice Baby' and the B-52's `Love Shack.''  _Mike Rogers, sports editor at the Orange (Texas) Leader. BILL'S WORLD  By Bill Tammeus, The Kansas City Star  Uncle Sam may run out of money soon. Yes, but the money he'll run out of is what he borrowed because he was out of money.  The U.N. birthday party was called the largest gathering of world leaders in history. By contrast, the recent Clinton-Yeltsin meeting was a gathering of the largest world leaders.  When Boris Yeltsin told the White House press corps, ``You're a disaster,'' the other day, Bill Clinton laughed so hard he cried. But notice he didn't assign FEMA to the case.  People in Quebec, who long have talked of splitting from Canada, vote Monday on it. The separatists' motto: ``If at first you don't secede. . .''  At a separatist rally in Quebec City the other night, people chanted, ``We want a country.'' Aren't pieces of the old Soviet Union, say, for sale?  ``There are no death squads in Cuba,'' Fidel Castro told New Yorkers the other day. Apparently this is what passes for good news there.  No doubt you will always remember exactly where you were and what you were doing when you heard that Pete Wilson had endorsed Bob Dole.  Thanksgiving turkeys this year will cost a bit more than they did last year, officials say. We report this so you may keep abreast.  Strong export demand for U.S. turkeys is keeping prices up, reports say. Is this a global _ or gobble _ economy?  The rocket that blew up just after launch from a NASA facility the other day was worth $20 million, it's reported. Which works out now to about a dollar a piece.  CITY STREETS  SCENE: A theater district boite.  Time: Late Saturday night, during the weekend of the United Nations celebration.  Dramatis personae: Three young male theater types, very likely dancers, discussing rumor and fact.  Fellow one: I heard Yeltsin was at ``Sunset'' tonight.  Fellow two: The Foreign Minister of Egypt and the King of Latvia were at ``Victor/Victoria.''  Fellow three, quietly: I saw my periodontist and his wife in Shubert Alley.  David Snyder, a native New Yorker who has been living in Baltimore, paid a recent return visit. In the lobby of the Empire State Building, a tourist asked him for directions to Madison Avenue. ``That way,'' Snyder said, pointing west. No sooner had the words left his mouth than he realized he had misguided her, but she was swallowed up in the tumult of 34th Street.  Several hours later, as Snyder strolled through Central Park, a vaguely familiar face stared at him steadily, and a voice said icily: ``Thanks a lot!''  You can't hide in New York.  Crowds lined up along West 42d Street waiting for Pope John Paul II to return from Giants Stadium through the Lincoln Tunnel. As the papal entourage came slowly into view, a buzz of excitement took hold and people began to wave and shout. As the Popemobile passed them, people smiled. The Pope seemed to be smiling, too, and it could have been because a group of diners from Chez Josephine fervently waved their oversize dinner napkins in his direction.  And then, he was gone and everyone stood in silence.  When the diners returned to their meal at Chez Jo, one of them approached Jean-Claude Baker, the proprietor.  ``We're not Catholic,'' she said, ``but we were so moved by the Pope that'' _a pause here _ ``well, may we keep our napkins?''  From Barbara Ball Buff:  I've always known that New York is a city of specialists. Just how special I didn't realize until the other afternoon.  I went to the Upper East Side market across the street from where I live, intending to give a bag of empty bottles and cans to the neighborhood collectors. This time none of them were there and the bottle-recycling machines were out of order. I wound up carrying the bags back across the street; there, I encountered a regular walking along with a large bag bulging with empty bottles. Holding open my bag of a dozen or so plastic bottles, I asked him, ``Would you like these?'''  Without pausing and with only a quick glance into the bag, he replied, ``I don't do that kind.''  After recently viewing the new Jurassic-size refurbishment of the dinosaur exhibition hall at the American Museum of Natural History, Dr. William G. Cahan, a cancer specialist as well as a fellow with an antic sense of humor, came up with several species.  The musical: Dinahshorus.  The literary: Brontesaurus and Rogetthesaurus.  Diary readers, take it from there.  GOOD DAY, WIRE WANDERERS!!!!!  Offer not available in any store! (Or any other news service!) HOOTIE AND THE BLOWFISH TAKE A VIEW ON CONTRACT LAW? YES BROWSING FOR PRESIDENTIAL CANDIDATES AMONG THE NEW BOOK RELEASES (ll)  c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  NEW YORK _ It was a packed house Wednesday as 350 students at the Benjamin N. Cardozo School of Law at Yeshiva University turned up for a class that has an enrollment of 22.  The draw was the panel of guest lecturers, which included Hootie and the Blowfish, the wildly popular rock group.  The four band members had been invited by MichaelReinert, a music industry executive who teaches a class in contract drafting and negotiation in the music industry.  Although the atmosphere during the discussion on the fine points of entertainment contracts was not quite that of the band's sold-out concerts, there was a casual air to the session.  ``Cracked Rear View,'' the band's multiplatinum album, was playing on the sound system as students arrived, and Reinert, a vice president of Polygram Holdings, presented each band member with a T-shirt bearing his name with the title ``Professor.''  But for most of the hour-and-a-half class, Hootie and the Blowfish _ Darius Rucker, Dean Felber, Mark Bryan and Jim (Soni)Sonefeld _ were serious indeed, explaining the business and legal arrangements they had forged since assembling as a band at the University of South Carolina in 1986.  ``The music is why we do it,'' said Rucker, the lead singer and a guitarist. ``But we knew that if we were going to make it, everyone on the business side needed to be taken care of.''  Their attorney, Richard Gusler of Raleigh, N.C., who was also on the panel, said he had initially resisted representing the group. ``They called two times, and I refused to see them because I didn't have time,'' he said. ``Plus, I'd gotten really frustrated with musicians in the past.''  In time he relented. ``The first thing they wanted to talk about was business structure,'' he said. ``They asked really very astute questions concerning business.''  c.1995 N.Y. Times News Service  The book circuit seems to produce much speculation these days about presidential politics, perhaps as a result of Colin Powell's recent flirtation with a candidacy on his book tour.  At a lecture Wednesday night in Arlington, Va., LawrenceGrossman, a former president of NBC News and PBS, remarked that during his recent book tour he had harbored some yearning for a vice-presidential nomination. He is the author of ``The Electronic Republic: Reshaping Democracy in the Information Age'' (Viking) and was addressing the Freedom Forum, a foundation devoted to first amendment issues.  ``But unlike Colin Powell, I couldn't get the slightest ground swell,'' he said. Then he added with a broad smile: ``I was really disappointed when he announced today that he's not running, because I think we would have made a great ticket. He's from the Bronx, I'm from Brooklyn. He's black, I'm white. He's Episcopalian and I'm Jewish.''  However, Ben Bradlee, the former executive editor of The Washington Post, who has also been on the road promoting his autobiography, ``A Good Life: Newspapering and Other Adventures'' (Simon &AMP; Schuster), was less sanguine about a presidential quest.  At a National Press Club luncheon Tuesday in Washington, Bradlee was asked how many books he would have to sell before considering a run for the presidency.  Without hesitation, he replied, ``There are not enough trees in the forest.'' </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19951109.0521 type=story > <HEADLINE> JAPANESE STOCKS MIXED; BRIGHT SONY EARNINGS OFFSET DOLLAR WOES </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> Tokyo, Nov. 10 (Bloomberg) (BC-JAPAN-STOCKS-BLOOM) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_199511.gz"

When asked if the veterans would get a break before the regular summer workouts, he replied, ``Yeah, about half a day.'' Well, a new agreement with the Players' Association will prevent Parcells from carrying out his plan. Under the new rules, no team can hold even a voluntary mini-camp within 10 days of the official start of training camp. ``It's a mandatory 10-day vacation,'' says NFLPA head Gene Upshaw ... The Patriots' front office is not the only one having power struggles. When the Eagles brought in Dick Daniels in February to head up their football operations, a battle began with college scouting director John Wooten,an ally of head coach Ray Rhodes. Daniels took over the draft, usurped much of Wooten's authority and caused a rift on draft day. He has since fired two area scouts, and word is Wooten is in trouble as well but Rhodes is battling to save him ... Bill Walsh has been heard but not seen since his return to the 49ers in January, and that's the way he intends to keep it. Walsh works in a spartan office on the second floor of the 49ers' complex in Santa Clara, Calif., seldom speaking with anyone but the offensive coaches with whom he meets weekly to discuss the evolution of the West Coast offense. Walsh, who helped perfect that offense, is retooling the 49ers' version for head coach George Seifert and offensive coordinator Marc Trestman as a consultant ... Dallas running back Emmitt Smith is not the only player returning to finish his college degree, although he drew the lion's share of publicity when he graduated from the University of Florida last week, six years after he left Gainesville for the NFL. Among the 400 athletes who have returned to school through the Pro Degree Completion Program of the National Consortium for Academics and Sport is Kansas City linebacker Derrick Thomas,who has been working on a bachelor's degree in criminal justice at Missouri-Kansas City. He is one of seven Chiefs working on their degrees, but Thomas is doing it differently than most: He attends classes in the offseason and during the season. The seven-time Pro Bowler attends practices and meetings, has dinner, then takes an evening class before often returning to Arrowhead Stadium around 10 p.m. to pick up additional film from his coaches ... Baltimore Ravens owner Art Modell has been true to at least a portion of his word. Although he abandoned Cleveland, he said he would leave the Browns' history behind. He has. Two truckloads of the team's memorabilia were moved to the Pro Football Hall of Fame in Canton, Ohio, where they will be stored until Cleveland gets an expansion franchise no later than 1999 ... Raiders owner Al Davis has always believed in No. 1 picks. He has rehabilitated many a failed one in the past, and this year the Raiders have four of the top 11 picks from the 1991 draft, none of whom was originally taken by Oakland. Times News Service  LEO BURNETT PROMOTES EXECUTIVES  Leo Burnett Co. in Chicago has expanded top management by promoting several executives as a leading creative executive leaves.  James M. Jenness, 50, group president for the Americas, was promoted to the new post of vice chairman and chief operating officer, reporting to William T. Lynch, president and chief executive. When Lynch, 53, was asked whether that made Jenness his heir apparent, he replied, laughing, ``I'm going to be around for a long time.''  Michael B. Conrad, 52, group president and deputy chief creative officer, was also promoted to vice chairman and will share the title of chief creative officer with Richard B. Fizdale, chairman. Fizdale, 56, said he intended to retire at the end of 1997. Conrad also assumes duties as chief creative officer for Burnett's American operations, succeeding Robert H. Welke, 49, who had been executive vice president and chief creative officer of Leo Burnett U.S.A.  The Chicago Tribune reported Friday that Welke was leaving for a senior creative post at Euro RSCG Tatham in Chicago. Mary Baglivo, chief client officer at Euro RSCG Tatham, which is owned by Havas Advertising, said her agency was talking with Welke.  Cheryl R. Berman, 43, executive vice president and group creative director, was promoted to the new post of deputy chief creative officer at Burnett U.S.A., reporting to Conrad. And Linda S. Wolf, 48, executive vice president, client service director and director for business development worldwide, was named president of Burnett U.S.A. and regional managing director for North America, assuming duties from Jenness.  UNIVERSAL STUDIOS NAMES AGENCY  The Universal Studios Recreation Group division of MCA Inc. in Universal City, Calif., has named two units of Foote, Cone &AMP; Belding to handle theme park advertising, an assignment that had been divided for four years between Seiniger Kaufman Advertising in Beverly Hills, Calif., which handled creative duties, and the DDB Needham Los Angeles unit of the Omnicom Group, which handled media. Billings were estimated at more than $20 million.  Jim Yeager, a spokesman for the recreation group, said Foote, Cone in Los Angeles would handle creative and media for the theme park in Universal City and FCB/Leber Katz Partners in New York would handle those tasks for the Orlando, Fla., park. MCA is controlled by Seagram Co. Foote, Cone is owned by True North Communications.  The assignments came after a six-month review that included Seiniger Kaufman, part of the Seiniger Advertising Group, which teamed up with D'Arcy Masius Benton &AMP; Bowles, a unit of the MacManus Group; the New York and Torrance, Calif., offices of Saatchi &AMP; Saatchi Advertising Worldwide, a unit of Cordiant PLC; the Los Angeles and New York offices of TBWA Chiat/Day, owned by Omnicom, and the Atlanta and San Francisco offices of J. Walter Thompson, part of the WPP Group. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960721.0321 type=story > <HEADLINE> A BANNER DAY FOR U.S. WOMEN ON SOFTBALL FIELD AND BEYOND </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> COLUMBUS, Ga. AUTHOR-THON (Undated) _ Serious writers are suddenly ``discovered,'' as is happening now to Melanie Rae Thon, at various points in their lives and careers. It can come about through pluck or luck, a well-orchestrated marketing campaign, one book's unexpected popularity, all sorts of occurrences or happenstances. But seldom does ``being discovered'' come at just the right moment, as is the case with Thon. PHOTO.  By John Marshall. (Story will move in the ``e'' category.) NYT News Service  INTERNATIONAL _ (Moved in ``i'' category)  SPAIN-IMMIG (Ceuta, Spain) _ More and more migrants from African countries like Nigeria, Senegal, Mali and Guinea, desperate for a better way of life, are taking a route once used almost exclusively by Moroccans. They board small and dangerously overcrowded boats hoping to cross the Straits of Gibraltar to the Spanish mainland and gain a foothold in Europe. The Spanish government, prompted by its partners in a Europe of dissolving internal borders, has been doing all it can to seal its southern frontier, but the tide of migrants from African countries is rising at an astounding pace. By Suzanne Daley. 1,700 words.  JAPAN-TEXTBOOKS (Tokyo) _ The Japanese government has said it will not revise new history textbooks, further infuriating South Korea and China, which say the books largely ignore World War II atrocities. By Howard W. French.  NATIONAL _ GENERAL (Moved in ``a'' category)  SCOTT-HERON-DRUGS (New York) _ Gil Scott-Heron, the musician, writer, spoken-word poet and activist reached a crossroads last week: The gaunt 52-year-old pleaded guilty to felony possession of cocaine and agreed to enter a treatment program in September. That moment of courtroom reckoning has inspired dismay among friends who see him as a victim of punitive drug laws, hope among others who want him to get help, but little surprise. This case, like that of Robert Downey Jr. or Darryl Strawberry, shows how fame adds a public cast to private disintegration. By Amy Waldman. 1,900 words.  (ART ADV: Photo NYT17 is being sent to NYT photo clients.)  WASHINGTON _ (Moved in ``w'' category)  MISSILE-DEFENSE (Washington) _ The Pentagon is preparing to ask Congress for funds to build a missile defense test site in Alaska that could also become the command center for a working anti-missile system as early as 2004, defense officials say. By James Dao. 1,200 words.  (ART ADV: Graphic is being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  MCCAIN-DONATE (Washington) _ On the eve of House debate on the McCain-Feingold campaign finance bill, a look at the think tank created by Sen. John McCain and others to further the cause of overhauling the way political campaigns are funded. By Philip Shenon.  (ART ADV: Photos NYT8,9 are being sent to NYT photo clients.)  CONGRESS-EDUC (Washington) _ Despite bipartisan shows of satisfaction as the House and Senate passed recent bills to require annual testing of all public school children, several of the most crucial issues _ including how states will measure progress and how strictly they will be held accountable for educating poor black and Hispanic students _ remain to be negotiated by a still-unnamed conference committee. By Diana Jean Schemo.  BUSH-STRATEGY (Washington) _ With his popularity slumping in opinion polls, President Bush is trying to regain momentum on two high-visibility items _ the patients bill of rights and his faith-based initiative. By Frank Bruni.  BUSINESS DAY _ (Moved in ``f'' category)  WEBVAN-BANKRUPTCY (Undated) _ Webvan, the online grocer that epitomized the buoyant optimism that the Internet could revolutionize even the most entrenched industries, closes and says it will seek bankruptcy protection. After spending almost all of the $1.2 billion put up by investors, Webvan ran out of money long before it could hope to turn a profit, and its search for a buyer or source of additional cash was fruitless. By Saul Hansell. 1,300 words.  COMCAST-CONSUMERS (Undated) _ A look at what consumers can expect if Comcast Corp. succeeds in acquiring AT&AMP;T's cable operation. By Simon Romero.  SOYBEANS-TRANSPORT (Itacoatiara, Brazil) _ The blazing midday sun dances off the long line of bright gold barges preparing to disgorge their cargo, Asia-bound soybeans, at this tiny but up-to-date Amazon River port. Meanwhile, 4,000 miles north of here, on the Mississippi River, 15-barge configurations loaded with Iowa- and Illinois-grown soybeans often wait in line for hours to pass through a series of creaky 80-year-old locks that will lower them down to the sea at New Orleans. In the race to get soybeans to the global marketplace, the United States, the longtime champ, is faltering. As they watch competitors like Brazil, Argentina and China invest heavily to reduce shipping time and costs, American farmers say that the river that once helped make United States agriculture the low-price leader is now costing them about $100 million a year in delays. Against objections from environmentalists and some economists, a farmers' group is clamoring for taxpayers to foot at least part of the bill of more than $1 billion to lengthen and upgrade the locks before it is too late. By Jennifer L. Rich.  (ART ADV: Map and graphic are being sent to NYT graphic clients.)  BROKERAGE-AD-COLUMN (New York) _ At a time when online trading volume is contracting faster than you can say ``Jackrobinson.com'' and most Web-based brokerage firms are cutting their advertising budgets, a new online brokerage firm is seeking to generate awareness among and business from potential customers with a television, print and online campaign. By Stuart Elliott.  COMMENTARY (Moved in ``k'' category)  FRIEDMAN-COLUMN _ Commentary by Thomas L. But people cannot help wondering if La Scala's management can keep its promise to reopen on Dec. 7, 2004. By Alan Riding.  STORY-TELLING (Undated) _ Storytelling as an urban art form has come a long way since 1997. That was the year that George Green, a novelist, first invited friends to his Manhattan apartment for an evening of swapping stories, in hopes of replicating the moth-infested, Jack Daniels-soaked yarns of his youth in Georgia. It took one night _ presumably a long one _ before Green realized that even the most inspired of storytellers require a little discipline and, in some cases, a lot of help. Such was the birth of the Moth, a loosely organized company that has since held regular storytelling evenings for sold-out crowds in bars, restaurants and even planetariums, mostly in New York but occasionally in Los Angeles or San Francisco. By Celestine Bohlen.  ORPHAN-BOOK-REVIEW (Undated) _ ``Yonder Stands Your Orphan'' is Barry Hannah's first novel in a decade, and that is a literary event. Two decades ago, Hannah announced himself on the scene with ``Geronimo Rex,'' a novel that made him an instant heir to the southern tradition of William Faulkner and Flannery O'Connor. Reviewed by Richard Bernstein.  (Editors: All clients receive all budgets, but only full-service clients receive all stories. (NYT27) NEW YORK -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- ECOMMERCE-COLUMN -- A Blockbuster video rental store on the Upper East Side of Manhattan. Patrons are being urged to sign up for monthly DVD subscription service with unlimited rentals. Blockbuster's subscription service is in the early stages, although company executives said last week that the effort was proceeding nicely. The service, called DVD Freedom Pass, allows store customers to rent an unlimited number of titles each month for between $20 and $25 a month, depending on the city. Customers may have up to three titles out at any time, and are not assessed late fees. (George M. Gutierrez/The New York Times)  -(NYT26) ALEXANDRIA, Va. -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- ANTHRAX-HATFILL-4 -- Dr. Steven J. Hatfill, a bioweapons expert under scrutiny for the anthrax attacks, during a news conference outside his lawyer's office in Alexandria, Va. Hatfill defended himself Sunday, complaining that leaks from the FBI are destroying his life while law enforcement officials have said Hatfill is one of about 30 scientists being looked at in the anthrax investigation. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)  -(NYT25) SHANGHAI, China -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- CHINA-CREMATION-3 -- Workers at the Yishan Crematory in Shanghai prepared to send a coffin to one of the facility's 24 incinerators, known as cremators. China began encouraging cremation decades ago when it became clear that the country could not continue burying people in the shrinking available land of its expanding cities. Today, about 8 million Chinese die each year and the number is creeping higher as the population ages. (R.S. Photos/The New York Times)  -(NYT24) WEEHAWKEN, N.J. -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- NY-NJ-FERRIES -- About 66,000 commuters cross the Hudson in ferries every weekday. At top, in foreground, the Lincoln Harbor Yacht Club in Weehawken, N.J., was a coveted place to dock boats. But the ferries' large wakes have sent some boaters elsewhere. Hewing to tight schedules, ferries speed across the water and throw wakes that snap lines, break up docks, throw people across their boats and generally wreak havoc on anyone trying to enjoy a quiet cruise, boaters and marina owners say. (Keith Meyers/The New York Times)  -(NYT23) NEW YORK -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- BABY-SIGN-LANGUAGE -- Kerith Spencer-Shapiro, at left, and her baby, Zev, and Cassie Stafford, and her child, Zeb, learn signing at a class in Brooklyn, N.Y. The class is one of a handful in the area teaching parents and infants as young as 6 months old to use rudimentary elements of sign language, though the infants can hear just fine. Experts differ on whether having children learn signs before they can speak is beneficial. (Nicole Bengiveno/The New York Times) - -(NYT22) SAINSHAND, Mongolia -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- MONGOLIA-MUSEUM -- Altangerel and his son, Altan-Ochir, are hereditary curators of the wealth of a 19th-century Mongolian incarnation of a Buddhist deity Danzan Ravjaa, also known as the Great and Horrible Saint of the Gobi, a Buddhist monk who was believed to be the 35th incarnation of Yansi Yadaam, a Mongolian deity. Despite the shortage of money and the clear need to improve conditions at the museum, Altangerel has refused to sell any part of the collection. ``My ancestors and the people of the Gobi have been protecting these artifacts for five generations,'' he says. Altangerel and his son at the Hamryn Hiid monastery in Mongolia. (Michael Kohn/The New York Times)  -(NYT21) SHANGHAI, China -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- CHINA-CREMATION-2 -- A worker at the Yishan Crematory in Shanghai, the city's only such facility, where the burn team ferries as many as 400 bodies a day to 24 incinerators, called cremators. Today, about 8 million Chinese die each year and the number is creeping higher as the population ages. (R.S. Photos/The New York Times)  -(NYT19) LONDON -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- BRITAIN-DRUGS-2 -- n South London, where a six-month experiment in loosening anti-marijuana laws has just ended, more youths are smoking openly. The program pleased Brixton's smokers, and even the police. But it left many residents feeling that their neighborhood had turned into an open-air drug bazaar, where teenagers brazenly smoke on the street and dealers set up shop next to fruit sellers in the market. Although the plan is an acknowledgment that drugs like heroin and cocaine are far more harmful than marijuana, the mixed reviews here raise a host of questions about loosening marijuana laws. The Rev. Chris Andre-Watson, of Brixton Baptist Church, says the experiment has left many ``zombied out.'' (Vincent Laforet/The New York Times) STAND ALONE PHOTO  -(NYT16) SEDALIA, Mo. -- Aug. 11, 2002 -- LIVESTOCK-CHEATING-3 -- At state and county fairgrounds across the United States, a plague of cheating in the mid-1990s forced leaders of the 4-H and the Future Farmers of America to concede that junior livestock competition in many cases was no longer good, clean, character-building fun. NEW YORK _  Dear Diary:  An entrepreneur was hawking a NordicTrack at Broadway and 82nd Street recently. With arms swinging and legs striding, he was demonstrating the machine while shouting its merits.  More surprising, he was offering the usually expensive exercise machine for only $25, delivered! He had many funny lines to attract onlookers. Two people engaged in a bidding war, with the winner claiming it for $40. Not a bad return for the intrepid peddler, considering I had seen that very contraption in the trash a block west earlier in the day.  _ TREY BARLOW  Dear Diary:  My downtown No. 1 train was jampacked. There wasn't even enough room to look down to see whose umbrella was flooding my pumps. At 79th Street the only things moving were the doors. Same for 72nd Street. At 66th, the doors opened to a man desperate to get on the train.  ``Doesn't anyone want to get off here?'' he asked. ``We've got Lincoln Center.'' No takers. ``The Metropolitan Opera.'' Still no takers. ``And the Chagall murals are beautiful,'' he continued. ``Even the station is very nice, no? Well then, inhale everyone. A little more. A lot more.''  And then he got on.  _ MEREDITH HALPERN  Dear Diary:  My nephew, Grice Whitely, and his wife, Erin, had, at one point, four children under the age of 5. They are both quite religious and protective of their children and had forbidden them to watch television except for PBS programs like ``Sesame Street'' and National Geographic specials.  At a family gathering, the children were asked to thank God for all their blessings. The first three children in turn went through litanies thanking God for Mommy, Daddy, Grandma, Grandpa, brothers and sisters. When it was 4-year-old Tyler's turn, he started slowly, trying hard to come up with something original. He thanked God for an uncle and an aunt and then, concluding on a triumphant note, said, ``and thank God for viewers like you.''  _ BUDD HOPKINS  GROWING UP IN NEW YORK  Growing up any place out of New York meant nothing to me  because my Daddy stuck a cigar in his mouth,  viewed the streets of our city and said,  Da woild ends in Hoboken,  like he spoke gospel and we believed him.  We didn't envy the kids on farms or some babbling brook  as we rowed the boat on the lake in Central Park or  ambled in museum halls on a Sunday afternoon.  The Grand Canyon didn't impress me.  I had the shelter of Wall Street that yielded treasures of Chinatown,  reading the translated signs, convinced I could speak Chinese,  if I wanted, or even Italian, munching pizza in Little Italy.  I was a beatnik in the Village, a jazz authority on 52nd Street,  an art critic, a fashion queen, a bargain hunter at Klein's,  a poet on the Square.  I was a New Yorker and the world was mine.  _ CAROL GREENBERG  Agnes Davidson still remembers that when she turned 65 some 20 years ago, she went to City Hall for a reduced-fare transit card. Armed with her pre-present-day MetroCard, she boarded a bus near her home in Greenwich Village expecting that because her hair was not yet gray, the driver would show some surprise.  ``This calls for a celebration, she told him lightheartedly. ``It's the first time I've used it.'' The driver replied matter-of-factly, ``Did you lose the last one?''  Dear Diary:  I had just come out of the Chase Manhattan bank branch next to Yankee Stadium and put the $250 I had withdrawn in the side pocket of my leather jacket. As I sprinted toward my place of employment, I heard people calling, ``Miss, Miss, you dropped your money.'' I came to a halt, turned around and saw my money literally blowing in the wind.  I ran back and started grabbing it out of the air while people around me handed me bills. Moments later I had all my money, scrunched up in my hand. What a relief! A man asked if I got it all and when I said I had, he replied, ``Great.'' Everyone else around was smiling, looking almost as pleased as I was.  _ CHRISTINA FRAGOLA  Dear Diary:  I had just started a walk on the Brooklyn Heights Promenade on a recent warm night when I noticed out of the corner of my eye a guy on a bike weaving left to right behind me, as if on an obstacle course. He rolled by very closely and just as he crossed in front of me, he leaned over and gave me a quick kiss on the cheek. I smiled for the rest of the walk.  _ DIANE TAYLOR  Dear Diary:  On a walk along the Upper West Side, my husband and I witnessed an exchange between a delivery man on a bicycle and an older couple walking together. While riding on the sidewalk, the delivery man yelled to the couple, ``Watch out!''  The startled couple, hearing the warning, turned and said, ``No, you watch out _ you're supposed to be on the road, not the sidewalk!''  The delivery man had barely managed to stop before the light turned red. When it changed to green, he started to make his getaway. With only seconds remaining to express his frustration, the older man raised his left hand and quickly spanked the delivery man's behind. The delivery man, perhaps reflecting on his unusual punishment, proceeded at a much slower pace.  _ DIANE DEMPSEY  WELL-READ  I am going to die someday without having ever tasted  McDonald's Big Mac, 7-Eleven Slurpee, Ben &amp; Jerry's Rocky Road  although I know these words, answer the daily crossword puzzle with them.  I am going to be buried someday without having ever worn  Badgley Mischka beaded chiffon, Bill Blass tweed, Oscar de la Renta silks  although I discern one label from the next in fashion runway photographs.  I am going to be eulogized someday without ever having lived in  Beverly Hills Beaux Arts, Greenwich, Conn., colonial,  Palm Beach tropical hacienda, although I identify one from the other  just from reading home publications.  Information is a wonderful thing.  _ SUSAN ROSENBAUM </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20020811.0163 type=story > <HEADLINE> THE GREAT AND HORRIBLE SAINT OF THE GOBI LIVES ON IN A MONGOLIAN </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> SAINSHAND, Mongolia  (BC-MONGOLIA-MUSEUM-1STLD) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200208.gz"

NEW YORK _  Dear Diary:  Noticing the colorful flowers planted along the Park Avenue divider the other day, I recalled an incident that occurred a number of years ago.  Sophia Loren, then a recent Academy Award winner for ``Two Women,'' her husband, the producer Carlo Ponti, and several others had apparently just come from the Waldorf-Astoria hotel.  I was hurrying uptown on the east side of the avenue. Their party crossed at the intersection and I was not far behind.  All of them had crossed the street except Loren, who had stopped a moment to admire the plantings. That's how I found myself standing next to the world-famous movie star.  ``Ms. Loren,'' I said, ``I hope you won't mind, but from now on I intend to tell all my friends that I was once stranded on an island with you.''  Whipping off her dark glasses and treating me to a smile that could have lighted all of Rome for a month, she replied, ``All right,'' and then, jabbing the glasses in my direction she added, ``Just don't tell Carlo.''  _ HARVARD HOLLENBERG  Dear Diary:  While walking home from dinner one night, a friend and I came upon a small park on Greenwich Avenue. There were lights and cameramen and two people in police uniforms _ obviously actors.  The park was closed for the evening because of the filming. In front of us were two homeless men, both with large, overflowing shopping bags in their hands.  As they approached the park and noticed the ``Closed'' sign, one said to the other, ``You know, they're supposed to give us 24 hours' notice.''  _ KATHY SCHLATTER  Dear Diary:  Whenever I'm in New York, I keep my eyes open for celebrities.  Recently I was in a restaurant and noticed a husky, very athletic-looking man seated at the table behind me. A short time later, two people came over and took his picture. Since this was the day of the New York celebration marking the opening of the National Football League season, I was certain he was some well-known football player, though I didn't know which one. As I was leaving the restaurant, I asked the waiter if he knew who the VIP was.  ``Oh yes,'' he said. ``He's the bus driver for a group of tourists eating here.''  To the tourists who had seen New York traffic, he was indeed a celebrity.  _ BERNARD M. MECHLOWITZ  Dear Diary:  On the path to the exit of the Brooklyn Botanic Garden, I noticed a little girl ahead of me, walking with her parents. Her mother seemed to be helping her stay upright. As I moved alongside the family, I saw that the child wasn't sick or handicapped, just very tired after a day at the Harvest Fair. The girl, about 5, was too big to carry easily, and her parents were trying to assure her that their car wasn't far away, even as she seemed about to drop from exhaustion.  I turned to the little girl and said, ``You look like you need an imaginary pony.'' Immediately, a look of understanding crossed her face; she smiled broadly, and to her parents' astonishment, she stood upright and began to trot. As her parents began to laugh with relief, I asked the little girl what she would like to name her pony. She thought for a moment and answered, ``Sally.'' I told her I thought that was a very good pony name.  As we reached the exit, the girl's father asked me if I worked in the field of child psychology. ``No,'' I answered. ``But when my children were young, we often took long walks, and I found that it was often helpful to bring along imaginary ponies for the way back.''  As our paths diverged, I noticed that the little girl had galloped about 30 feet ahead of her parents, happily astride Sally.  _ LYN S. HILL  Dear Diary:  I've seen a young woman sleeping on a bare, stained mattress in my East Village neighborhood for the past month or so. It's on my usual route home from the gym, so I've had a chance to see her open-air accommodations many times. Sometimes food wrapped in napkins and open bottles of water and juice have sat next to the mattress when she was not home.  I assumed that at least some of these were donated by charitable New Yorkers. Recently I saw the mattress transformed into a bed, complete with pillow. It was made more carefully than my own, with a pristine bottom sheet and crisp green pillow case. Whether this was her doing or a generous display of compassion and hospitality, I couldn't be sure. What was most striking was the tiny brown teddy bear resting in the center of the pillow.  _ CAROL FORGET  Dear Diary:  My aunt was recently at her local supermarket deli department in Glen Cove on Long Island to order her usual turkey breast. She overheard the woman next to her ordering a special brand of turkey breast. Curious, she asked the woman if she thought that brand was really so much better.  ``I wouldn't know,'' the woman replied. ``I've never tried it. I buy it for my dog.''  _ AMY ZELENETZ KORNFELD  Dear Diary:  A few years ago, my teenage son and I were returning to Connecticut after dropping off my wife at Kennedy Airport. I suggested we go through the city and said I'd treat him to his favorite dessert at Planet Hollywood. I wasn't sure exactly where it was, so when a cab stopped next to me at a red light, I lowered my window and asked for directions.  The cabby smiled, held out his hand and said, ``Three dollars.''  ``Three dollars,'' I said. ``Is that any way to make a visitor feel welcome?''  Whereupon the rear window of the cab rolled down, and a nicely dressed woman leaned out and yelled, ``Turn right here and take a left on 57th. It'll be on your left.''  I thanked her, smiled at the cabdriver and turned to my son. ``That,'' I said, ``was a New York moment.''  _ GARY MICHAELS  Dear Diary:  On my way home from doing some shopping, I noticed a huge crane in front of a brownstone on my block. Curious, I watched to see what they were doing. A man who was obviously in charge must have thought I was concerned about the crane falling. With a charming smile, he said:  ``You needn't cast your gaze on high.  I will see that you get safely by.''  I love this town!  _ JUDY NEGRON </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20020929.0202 type=story > <HEADLINE> FUND FOR TERROR ATTACK VICTIMS OFFERS 14 AWARDS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> NEW YORK  (BC-WTC-VICTIMS-FUND-645&amp;) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200209.gz"

(NYT40) LAS VEGAS -- July 9, 2005 -- POKER-BRUNSON-2 -- Doyle Brunson was the host of Hollywood stars and models at a glitzy soiree before he competed in the 36th annual World Series of Poker in Las Vegas on Friday, July 9, 2005. Brunson is the old-school rounder who in the 1950s and '60s was held up at gunpoint in backrooms from Fort Worth to Austin, from Laredo to Lubbock, but now at age 71 only has to stare down battalions of television cameras. In 1978 he wrote what is widely regarded as poker's bible, Super System: A Course in Power Poker. It still sells 10,000 copies a month and no doubt has been thumbed through by many of the more than 5,600 players here for the main event of the 36th annual World Series of Poker -- no-limit Texas Hold'em. For the latest photos and graphics from The New York Times, go to www.nytimages.com.  INTERNATIONAL (i code)  MIDEAST (Jerusalem) -- A ledeall. Israel called up a few thousand reservists on Friday, in possible preparation for a more extensive ground operation in southern Lebanon, as its warplanes continued to hit targets there and to drop leaflets warning residents of villages to leave their homes and head northward. Over the last two days, there has been an increase in ground clashes as Israeli troops have moved about a mile or so inside Lebanon to demolish Hezbollah outposts and fortifications. They have encountered fierce resistance. Four Israeli soldiers were killed in battles with Hezbollah guerrillas north of Avivim on Thursday, the Israeli army said. All day Friday, the 10th day of the conflict, Israeli jets continued to drop bombs, hitting Beirut's Shiite districts, the eastern Bekaa Valley and southern Lebanon and striking the Mdeirej bridge on the main Beirut-Damascus highway four more times; it had already been bombed twice. Other deelopments. By Greg Myre and Jad Mouawad.  With photos.  An early lede has moved.  MIDEAST-DIPLO (Washington) -- U.S. Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice said she would travel to the Middle East on Sunday but dismissed growing pressure for an immediate ceasefire between Israel and Hezbollah guerrillas in Lebanon. She said she would visit Israel and the Palestinian territories and join Arab officials at an international conference on Lebanon in Rome. Rice told a news conference she hoped her trip would create conditions for a lasting Middle East peace but the diplomatic work would be difficult. (Summary from wires) By Helene Cooper.  MIDEAST-DAMASCUS (Damascus, Syria) -- From the Damascus airport, fleeing people feel they are without a country. By Sabrina Tavernise.  MIDEAST-ARABS (Damascus, Syria) -- Looking at Friday prayers across the region. Despite the official anti-Hezbollah line, various sermons in Egypt, Saudi, Jordan, etc. called for the victory of Hezbollah and Muslims in general. By Neil MacFarquhar.  MIDEAST-CYPRUS (Larnaca, Cyprus) -- Thousands of expatriates arrive from Lebanon with tales to tell. By Renwick McLean.  IRAQ (Baghdad, Iraq) -- Developments, including a report that only 28 people were killed across the country on Friday, a decline from the daily averages. By Paul von Zielbauer.  With  IRAQ-ABUSE (Baghdad, Iraq) -- The scene in the military courtroom in Iraq where Article 32 hearings are being held for Americans accused of crimes against Iraqi civilians. By Paul von Zielbauer.  SOMALIA (Kinshasa, Congo) -- Developments as Ethiopia, defending the interim Somalia government, and Islamists face off over who will rule Somalia. By Jeffrey Gettleman.  PAKISTAN-MEDIA (Islamabad, Pakistan) -- Pakistan, which has liberalized its attitude toward the media, is pressing news organizations not to use gruesome images. By Salman Masood.  RUSSIA-FRAUD (Moscow) -- A former Russian nuclear energy minister who is wanted in the United States on federal conspiracy and money laundering charges was released from prison on bail Friday by Russia's Supreme Court. The former minister, Yevgeny O. Adamov, has been the subject of a long legal tug-of-war between Russia and the United States, which accused him of diverting about $9 million in American aid for nuclear security to shell companies and personal accounts. His release ended for the moment an extended period of incarceration in two countries, but did not appear to end his legal difficulties in Russia, where he has been charged with abuse of office and fraud. The United States also said that its charges against him are still pending. By C.J. Chivers.  INDIA-TERROR (New Delhi, India) -- The police in Mumbai, India's financial capital, announced on Friday the arrests of three men they said were being questioned in connection with last week's serial bombings, but offered no details about the nature of their involvement. Since the July 11 bombings on Mumbai's commuter train line, the police have questioned hundreds of people but today was the first time arrests were made. By Hari Kumar.  CLASSIFIED-BLOGS (Washington) -- A classified intelligence network allows government employees with security clearances to exchange information and observations via their own blogs. Now one employee has been fired, for using her blog to suggest that interrogation techniques used by the CIA against high-value terror suspects amounted to torture. A look into the world of classified blogs, which may be a contradiction in terms. By Mark Mazzetti.  LEAVITT-TAXES (Washington) -- The secretary of health and human services, Mike Leavitt, and some of his family have claimed millions of dollars in tax deductions through a charitable foundation they created that has paid out little in actual charity. Instead, it appears that much of the foundation's money has been invested or diverted to the family's business interests and real estate holdings. By Robert Pear.  NATIONAL GENERAL (a code)  NEW-ORLEANS-POWER (New Orleans) -- To the list of daily aggravations in the new New Orleans, add one that augments the heat, spoils the food, and drains the cash register: power outages. The wind blows and a neighborhood's power goes out. Or it rains and the power goes out somewhere else. Thunder crashes distantly and out goes the power. Ten months after Hurricane Katrina, the city still does not have a reliable electrical system. Hundreds of millions in repairs are still needed on an electrical system devastated by flooding, the local utility is in bankruptcy, and less than half its pre-storm customers have returned. Of those that have, many have endured hot and sleepless nights with no air conditioning. So much for the injury; now comes the insult. Entergy, the power company here, wants to increase its rates 25 percent to help pay for part of what it says is a $718 million shortfall. By Adam Nossiter.  MA-GAYS-MARRIAGE (Boston) -- The lesbian couple who were the lead plaintiffs in the lawsuit that made gay marriage legal have decided to separate. By Katie Zezima.  FINANCIAL (f code)  SEC-MACK (Undated) -- John J. Mack, who now runs Morgan Stanley, testifies before the Securities and Exchange Commission in the investigation into Pequot Capital Management, a prominent hedge fund suspected of insider-trading violations. An SEC investigator, Gary J. Aguirre, told Congress last month that SEC officials gave in to political pressure and stopped his investigation after he tried to take Mack's testimony. By Gretchen Morgenson.  HIDDEN-FEES (Undated) -- When Xavier Gabaix and David Laibson open a hotel room minibar, they see among the tiny liquor bottles and European chocolates a perpetual battle between companies charging hidden fees and the sophisticated consumer trying to avoid them. The two economics professors have looked at how companies hide fees and costs. They found that sophisticated consumers have somehow learned how to game the system by having enough nave consumers around to subsidize them. The smartest strategy, they say, is for the sophisticated consumer to choose the service with the most hidden charges and highest add-on prices, but then avoid paying those added costs. The weekly Money feature by Damon Darlin.  NOCERA-COLUMN (Undated) -- How a company called BKF Asset Management was decimated by some hedge funds that took control of it in the name of shareholder activism. Mug of John A. Levin, who owned the firm prior to BKF. The weekly column by Joe Nocera.  CHINA-BANK (Beijing) -- China's central bank on Friday tightened lending restrictions on banks for the second time in about a month, as it tries to slow excessive credit and investment growth. By John Lague.  DELL-OUTLOOK (Undated) -- Dell Inc. warned that second-quarter earnings and revenue would fall substantially short of forecasts, blaming what it called aggressive pricing in a slowing market. The warning is latest sign that the PC market, and the tech market as a whole is struggling. By Laurie J. Flynn.  ENRON-BANKERS (Houston) -- Three British bankers facing Enron-related fraud charges must reside in the United States pending trial, a judge ruled. U.S. Magistrate Stephen Smith sided with federal prosecutors in granting a bond that blocks the trio from returning to England and possibly launching another lengthy extradition battle after losing a string of appeals over more than two years. By Kate Murphy.  KOTICK-VIDEO-GAMES (Undated) -- Every four or five years, the video game industry hits a mammoth cyclical downturn as consumers wait for new gaming consoles. Robert Kotick, the chairman and chief executive of Activision since 1991, has experienced four cycles, including the current one. His company, the second-largest public company that publishes video games, has, like its peers, been hammered in the stock market. By weekly Q&amp;A interview, by Matt Richtel.  U.S.-DEBT (Undated) -- Only two years after foreign central banks had become virtually the only net buyer of U.S. Treasury securities -- and thus the financier for U.S. budget deficits -- these banks have turned into net sellers of securities. The Treasury reported this week that foreign central banks were net sellers of Treasuries in May, as they had been in March. But other foreigners -- whose investments presumably reflect a search for profit rather than the central bank's desire to prop up the dollar -- bought a record billion of such securities. A Short Off the Charts feature by Floyd Norris. CULTURE AND ENTERTAINMENT (e code) LONDON-THEATER (London) -- Ben Brantley offers a Critic's Notebook on new plays in London. MUSEUMS-REINSTALL (Paris) -- Pity the curator in the age of the blockbuster. While art museums are usually rated by the quality of their permanent collections, it is all too often their temporary shows that stir excitement and draw crowds. Not infrequently, a work of art that is barely noticed while on permanent display is suddenly lionized in a short-term exhibition. One answer is to make the permanent collection seem, well, sexier. And to this end, some leading museums of modern and contemporary art are testing a fresh approach: If collections are frequently rearranged, either by bringing works out of storage or by changing the focus of installations, they can acquire something of the buzz of temporary shows.shows.  MUSEUM-ADMISSIONS (Undated) -- A Critic's Notebook by Roberta Smith: In a recent panel discussion, Glenn D. Lowry, director of the Museum of Modern Art, remarked that it is almost a moral duty that museums should be free. Another panelist, Philippe de Montebello, director of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, emphatically differed. Noting that people pay huge amounts to money to attend rock concerts and sports events, he asked, What is it about art that it shouldn't be paid for? That excellent question is front and center in New York, now the Met has announced a rise in its suggested admission fee from $15 to $20.  TV-PBS-CENSOR (Undated) -- Elizabeth Jensen writes about a new PBS policy that requires producers to pixelate the mouths of those who use language that is deemed unacceptable, thus making it impossible for viewers to read lips of those using bleeped-out language.  MINA-NOVEL (New York) -- Scottish detective fiction, or Tartan Noir as it's called, with its brooding sensibility, brutal humor and fixation on the nature of guilt and punishment, has more in common with the Russian novel than it does with traditional detective writing. And Denise Mina, author of The Dead Hour, published this month by Little, Brown &amp; Co., is one of a crop of contemporary Scottish writers, like Ian Rankin, Val McDermid, Louise Welsh and Christopher Brookmyre, who have made detective fiction Scotland's best-known literary form. A profile by Dinitia Smith. CYC-TOUR-DE-FRANCE (Macon, France) -- In cycling, the time trial is known as the race of truth, where each rider goes by himself as fast as he can, with only the clock as an opponent. On Saturday, all eyes will be on the clock as Floyd Landis and the other contenders in the Tour de France battle it out in a 35.3-mile sprint that winds through the French wine country from Le Creusot to Montceau-les-Mines. Landis spent much of the last week of the Tour de France predicting that the three-week race would come down to the final time trial. Because the final stage, a 95-mile ride that finishes with eight laps on the Champs-Elysees in Paris, is largely ceremonial until the final circuits, the rider in first place after the time trial is likely to win the Tour de France. Landis has also been saying that if that is the case, he likes his chances. By Edward Wyatt.  GLF-ANDERSON-COLUMN (Hoylake, England) -- Sports of The Times column on the British Open. By Dave Anderson.  GLF-BRITISH-OPEN (Hoylake, England) -- Tiger Woods surged into the lead of the British Open at Royal Liverpool Golf Club on Friday, firing a 7-under-par 65 to hold a one-shot cushion over Ernie Els midway through the tournament. Chris DiMarco stood three shots behind Woods on a warm day on the Wirral Peninsula. Woods holed out for an eagle, his second in as many days, with a 4-iron from the middle of the 14th fairway that bounced three times before smacking the flagstick and disappearing into the hole for a 2. By Damon Hack.  DUKE-LACROSSE (Durham, N.C.) -- Duke University has hired John Danowski from Hofstra to coach its embattled men's lacrosse team, the new coach said Friday. Danowski, whose son Matt is a Duke senior and All-America attackman for the Blue Devils, told The Associated Press that he will replace Mike Pressler, who resigned soon after a woman told police she was raped by three men at a March team party where she was hired to perform as a stripper. A grand jury has indicted three lacrosse players on charges of rape, kidnapping and sexual offense. Defense attorneys have strongly proclaimed the players' innocence. (Summary from wires) By Pete Thamel.  BKO-USA-COACH (Las Vegas) -- Duke Coach Mike Krzyzewski has flirted with NBA jobs before, but he always decided to stick with the coach-dominated world of college basketball. But now, USA Basketball has asked Krzyzewski to tackle one of the most difficult pro jobs, turning a bunch of NBA millionaires into a team that can master the international game and win an Olympic gold medal. Krzyzewski begins this job at a USA Basketball training camp in Las Vegas to prepare for next month's world championships in Japan, but his goal is to return the American team to gold medal contender status in 2008 after its embarrassing third-place finish in 2004. By Lynn Zinser.  FBC-AUBURN (Undated) -- Developments in the wake of charges that some members of Auburn's football team were able to sign up for directed study courses where they received high marks but were required to do little work. By Pete Thamel.  BOX-GATTI-BALDOMIR (Hoboken, N.J.) -- Arturo Gatti's penchant for brutal fights has made him a valuable commodity, one of the most popular and resilient boxers of the past decade. One untimely defeat can often derail a boxer's career, but Gatti remains a star despite seven losses, constantly coming back like a boomerang in shorts. Gatti has made 19 appearances on HBO, many of them bloody battles that featured knockdowns, punishment, and furious action that looked more like a bar room brawl than a prize fight. All 12,380 seats have been sold at Atlantic City's Boardwalk Hall for Saturday night's 12-round title fight between Gatti (40-7) and WBC welterweight champion Carlos Baldomir (42-9-6). By Clifton Brown.  RUN-ULTRAMARATHON (Undated) -- Scott Jurek, 32, the pre-eminent American ultramarathoner, will be back in Death Valley on Monday to defend his title in the Badwater Ultramarathon, a race that covers 135 miles over 13,000 vertical feet from Death Valley, Calif., to the base of Mount Whitney.
